(NaturalNews) Industry-funded flu vaccine studies are more likely to be published in prestigious journals and to be frequently cited in scientific literature, according to a survey of 274 studies conducted by researchers from the Cochrane Vaccine Field in Italy and published in a journal. A good study methodology had minimal impact on such factors. The study also found that few studies provided strong evidence of the flu vaccine's effectiveness. Typically, a higher quality study should be published in a more prestigious journal and should be cited more often. However, the researchers found no correlation between study quality and either of those factors. Instead, having a study partially or fully funded by the industry was the most effective way to increase a study's prominence. Overall, the authors found that most flu vaccine studies were of poor quality, with only 18% of them reporting conclusions that were actually supported by their findings (concordance). Researcher Tom Jefferson commented, "The study shows that one of the levers for accessing prestigious journals is the financial size of your sponsor. Pharmaceutical sponsors order many reprints of studies supporting their products, often with in-house translations into many languages. They will also purchase publicity space on the journal. Many publishers openly advertise these services on their Web site." Jefferson concluded, "It is time journals made a full disclosure of their sources of funding." The researchers also found that studies supporting the effectiveness of flu vaccines tended to be more poorly designed than studies not supporting their effectiveness. Concordance was also significantly lower in studies supporting vaccine effectiveness than in those refuting it - meaning that most studies failed to support the effectiveness of the flu vaccine. Government-funded studies were significantly less likely to find the flu vaccine effective than industry-funded studies. Sources for this story include: health.usnews.com.
During a confirmation hearing on January 10, Senator Jeff Sessions, the nominee for attorney general, was questioned by Senator John Kennedy (Republican from Louisiana) regarding his views on the Second Amendment. Kennedy inquired, "In Louisiana, Senator, we believe that love is the answer. However, we also believe that we have the right under the Constitution to own a gun, just in case. Could you share your thoughts on the Second Amendment?" Sessions responded, "I do believe the Second Amendment is a personal right, a historic right of the American people. The Constitution protects it and explicitly states it. It is as important as any other rights and liberties we value." Sessions further explained that the right to bear arms can be revoked for those who break the law. He stated, "Individuals can lose their right to own a gun, and this can be a factor in sentencing and prosecution if, for instance, a person carries a gun during the commission of a crime. This can result in additional penalties and convictions. I think this is a reasonable and responsible limitation on the Second Amendment right to bear arms."
In White House, Tennessee, 19-year-old Garrett Preston shares his harrowing experience after a vape mod explosion left him with severe burns on his stomach, knee, and leg. Witnessing the incident firsthand, Preston hopes to warn others about the potential risks. During his first full shift at Dunkin Donuts last September, around 10 p.m., Preston was preparing a sandwich in the store's oven. In his left pocket were his car keys, phone, and an IPV 3 box mod, a device used for vaping. As he prepared the sandwich, three seconds into the process, a "fountain of fire" erupted from his pocket. Rushing to the back of the store, Preston removed the remains of his pants and saw his keys and vaporizer "smoking" on the floor. A co-worker soon appeared to check on him, and upon seeing his burns, called another employee to dial 911. Rushed to Vanderbilt's Burn Center, Preston was treated for two third-degree burns on his upper thigh and knee, as well as a severe burn to his stomach. After 14 hours in the hospital, he was discharged, but his recovery took a month and an additional week before doctors determined he no longer needed a skin graft. Preston believes he knows the reason for the vape box explosion. "I just want to share my experience, so that other people don't make a mistake and something worse happens to them. I want to promote safety about it. These vape batteries hold about four volts and could have done serious damage to someone's face," he said. Preston's vape box was missing the back case that covers the battery, and he suspects his keys touching the exposed batteries caused them to "ground out" and ignite a fire. Although the batteries are usually wrapped, cuts in the battery wrapping could expose the batteries and bring the potential for danger. The battery case fell off due to loose screws, a common issue for users who change the wattage in that area of the vape with the batteries. This issue has become more pressing with the retirement of highly-rated 49ers linebacker Chris Borland, who announced his decision at the age of 24 due to health concerns. The Federal Reserve stunned many today when its Open Market Committee revealed at 2 p.m. that it would not scale back its $85 billion in monthly bond purchases, citing economic weakness as the reason. This announcement sparked a widespread rally as investors rushed to invest in stocks and commodities. The market response was swift: According to data from my Bloomberg terminal, between 1:59 p.m. and 2:00 p.m., the Dow Jones Industrial Average surged 141 points, from 15,485 to 15,626. Over the following two and a half hours, the Dow added an additional 50 points to close the day with a gain of 147 points. On a more serious note, while the market and most people are celebrating, I view the situation differently. While the majority interprets the Fed's decision as "The Fed will not reduce $85 billion per month stimulus," I see it as "The Fed cannot reduce $85 billion per month stimulus." There is a significant distinction between these two statements. You see, Ben Bernanke and the Fed are aware that if they halt the QE bond purchasing program, certain events will unfold within a short period of time. Interest rates will rise. The dollar will strengthen. Deflation will become evident. The stock market will plummet. The real estate market and auto sales will decline. The US economy will struggle. They are keen to prevent these outcomes for obvious reasons. However, the laws of physics cannot be circumvented. Eventually, all of the aforementioned events will occur, regardless of the Fed's actions. I find it concerning that the market only rose by around 150 points. That is a relatively weak performance given the circumstances. In no uncertain terms, the FED has signaled that it will prolong this credit-financed speculation party for as long as possible. The biggest mistake I believe everyone is making is the assumption that the FED has control over the markets. That is far from the truth. They do not control anything. Don't be surprised if the market experiences a reversal tomorrow and begins its next downturn. Did you find this article interesting? Excessively aggressive medical interventions by doctors are a significant contributor to mortality rates following a heart attack or heart failure. These interventions, which include angioplasty and clot-dissolving medications, can potentially double the risk of death for a patient who has already survived these conditions. A study conducted by researchers from the Quebec Healthcare Assessment Agency in Montreal has revealed that the timing of these interventions is crucial. Ideally, these treatments should be initiated promptly upon the patient's admission to the hospital. Delays, even as short as a few minutes, can be fatal. The study found that approximately 68% of angioplasty procedures and 54% of clot-dissolving drug administrations were performed beyond the optimal timeframe, leading to an increased risk of death for these patients. (Source: Journal of the American Medical Association, 2010; 303: 2148)

A direct photograph of a fleeting and minuscule Higgs boson remains elusive, yet physicists can capture images of its relatives, focusing on atomic structures to enhance our comprehension of atomic physics. Recently, a groundbreaking image has surfaced: the first-ever snapshot of a single atom's shadow, the smallest object visible to the naked eye via light. A team spearheaded by Dave Kielpinski at Griffith University in Australia devised a method to trap a solitary ytterbium ion in an electric field, maintaining its stationary position for photographing. They illuminated it with light of a specific frequency and utilized an ultra-high-resolution microscope to focus on it. The atom cast a shadow onto a CCD detector, which recorded the image you see here. The team aimed to establish the number of atoms required to cast a shadow, and surprisingly, they discovered that only one was necessary. This discovery has potential practical applications, such as identifying the types of light necessary to observe specific biological processes without causing damage. The research will be published in Nature Communications this week.

A notable red flag that a company is on the verge of collapse is when its CEO becomes defensive towards those who question his vague assumptions about the financials. Frequently, these CEOs are attempting to conceal unattractive or inconvenient aspects by bullying analysts with a display of unwavering certainty. Essentially, they are saying, "I've succeeded in the past, so I'll succeed again. Trust me without question." In essence, "stop pressing me for explanations, you insignificant underling, and simply accept my word." CEOs who have nothing to conceal, on the other hand, are generally open to providing comprehensive explanations for their confidence. They won't squander time trying to persuade hardened skeptics, but they will certainly acknowledge that it is an analyst's duty to be critical, and they will disclose as much detail as possible. During the second presidential debate, moderator Candy Crowley hinted at the possibility that Romney's budget numbers might not align. His defensive response reminded me of the typical obstinate CEO whose company is on the brink of failure. Given the extraordinary feats that Romney claims his budget will achieve — reducing taxes, closing loopholes, not increasing the deficit, stimulating growth, increasing military spending — it is not surprising that most Americans are skeptical. So, what is it that gives Romney confidence that his numbers align? Why won't he disclose some of those numbers with the public? If Romney were a CEO of a public company, the conclusion many astute investors would draw is, "Romney refuses to disclose the numbers because he knows they don't align." Here is the transcript: Candy Crowley: "...If somehow the numbers don't add up, would you be willing to look again at a 20% (tax cut)" Mitt Romney: "Well of course they add up. I was someone who ran businesses for 25 years and balanced the budget. I ran the Olympics and balanced the budget. I ran the state of Massachusetts as a governor ... and balanced the budget all four years."

In recent exploration, I encountered a stepped-triangular (ST) approximation that can be realized as a sequence of recursive running sum (RRS) filters. This article provides a brief overview of the stepped-triangular approximation. The stepped-triangular approximation, initially proposed by Jovanovic-Dolecek and Mitra [1], serves as a quantized representation of a low-pass filter (LPF). Figure 1 illustrates an example of this approximation. [Figure 1: Stepped Approximation of a LPF Impulse] The original paper [1] detailed a method for the stepped-triangular approximation of a LPF impulse response. The resulting transfer function is a cascade of recursive sums. The H_rrs(z) is the familiar recursive running sum, which features a flat impulse response (Figure 2). [Figure 2: Impulse Response Recursive Running Sum] H_rss(z) represents a recursive sparse sum where every s samples are utilized (Figure 3). Both can be implemented efficiently with a recursive form that combines an integrator and difference. [Figure 3: Impulse Response Recursive Sparse Sum] To generate the stepped-triangular approximation, a simple rounding function g[n] is employed on the LPF impulse response. This rounding function quantizes h[n] into one of three stepped-triangular approximation types, depending on the number of samples at the peak (less than s, equal to s, or greater than s, where s is the number of samples per step). Figures 1 and 4 show an example of this. [Figure 4: Frequency Response for an ST-Approximation] Figure 4 demonstrates an example of a ST-approximation frequency response. Readers are encouraged to evaluate whether this approximation is suitable, considering the computational efficiency benefits. The blue line represents the original frequency response, while the red line shows the approximation using the stepped-triangular approach. [1] Jovanovic-Dolecek, Gordana, Mitra, Sanjit, “Design of FIR Lowpass filters using stepped triangular approximation”
Netflix has unveiled at least 34 original films this year, 2017, with plans to release 80 more in the subsequent year, as stated by the company's Chief Content Officer, Ted Sarandos. This number surpasses the output of most movie studios in a single year. For instance, Disney, which owns Pixar, Marvel Studios, and Lucasfilm, released 13 movies in the US in 2016. Warner Bros., New Line, Fine Line, Warner Independent, and Picturehouse studios combined put out 23 films, while Sony, with the highest count, released 38 movies in 2016. In comparison, Netflix's proposed release is nearly double that of Sony. This significant increase in Netflix's film releases is in contrast to the decline in output at major US movie studios since the golden age of Hollywood. In the 1920s to the 1940s, it was common for a studio's feature release slate to exceed 50 or even hundreds of films. However, with increased competition from television and streaming, and rising ticket prices, US studios today rely on fewer, larger releases. Countries like India have a higher rate of feature-film production, but the US still leads in box-office size. Netflix is bolstering its already substantial content budget to accommodate its ambitious movie-release schedule. The company anticipates spending between $7-8 billion on content overall next year, an increase from $6 billion in 2017, according to Netflix's Chief Financial Officer, David Wells. The films Netflix will release in 2018 will vary from lower-budget acquisitions, such as those purchased at Sundance, to large-scale productions typically destined for box-office runs. The company also has more films in the pipeline, including Martin Scorsese's next mobster film, The Irishman, scheduled for 2019. As the year draws to a close, Netflix is ending 2017 with its most expensive film to date, setting the stage for its aggressive film push in the following years. The incident took place at Skydive Perris, located at 2091 Goetz Road, as per the coroner's office. Deputies were dispatched to the area around Mapes and Goetz roads at approximately 7:00 a.m. due to an "air emergency." Upon arrival, they discovered Stone, who had sustained injuries from a fall. He was rushed to a hospital but unfortunately succumbed to his injuries later. Skydive Perris was hosting a national parachute competition on the said dates, which continued on Sunday. An official from Skydive Perris confirmed that Stone was not participating in the show and declined to comment on whether he was a skydiver. The Sheriff's Department has no additional information to share at this time. This story is currently evolving, and updates will be provided as they become available.
Approximately 2 million children are at risk of losing their health coverage starting next month if Congress fails to renew funding for the Children's Health Insurance Program (CHIP) by this Friday. According to state administrators of CHIP, parents are already panicking, and irreversible damage has been inflicted upon the program. CHIP provides health insurance for 9 million children nationwide. Congress failed to authorize funding in September, and some states have already started alerting parents to schedule their last medical visits while funds last. Cathy Caldwell, director of the Alabama Bureau of Children's Health Insurance, stated that their phones are ringing non-stop, with families in a state of panic trying to figure out their options. The crisis has left health policy experts, doctors, and politicians bewildered, as CHIP is a universally popular program that has never been used as a political pawn before. On Wednesday, Democrats in Congress urged their GOP colleagues, who control both the House and Senate, to act by Friday. "This is the ultimate Grinch-like story," Rep. Jackie Speier, D-Calif., said at a news conference where politicians held lumps of coal. CHIP has bipartisan support, but Republicans have been focused on passing a tax reform bill — their first legislative victory for 2017. "Congress must pass CHIP before they go on holiday," said Joan Alker, executive director of the Georgetown University Research Center. "Families need the assurance that their child's coverage will not expire as the new year begins." The report finds that 20 states will run out of money in the first quarter of 2018. "Never before has Congress let CHIP funding lapse for this long a period of time," Alker said. The continuing resolution that Congress passed earlier this month shifts around $1.24 billion in CHIP funds, so that the states running out of funds fastest get some 2017 money. However, this comes at the expense of states that were not running out as quickly. In essence, the patch robs Peter to pay Paul. The remaining 31 states will see their share of redistribution funds reduced, and thus the timeline by which they will run out of money is accelerated. Despite the additional funds, the 20 'winner' states will only have sufficient funds to operate their programs through January.
Numerous individuals burdened by private student loan repayments might find relief, as approximately $5 billion of such debt appears to lack necessary documentation for enforcing payments. Creditors attempting to recover past due debts have encountered obstacles in courts, due to inadequate ownership records and faulty documentation, according to The New York Times. The National Collegiate Student Loan Trusts, a significant holder of private student loan debt, has allegedly failed to provide legal documentation proving ownership of over $5 billion in student loans. This predicament has led to numerous debts being dismissed in court. For instance, Samantha Watson, a 33-year-old mother of three, faced court over a $31,000 student loan debt. However, the judge dismissed her debt when it was discovered that the National Collegiate failed to establish the chain of title on the loans, as reported by the Times. Similar cases in Texas, New Hampshire, and Ohio have been dismissed due to missing or flawed paperwork.
White House spokesman Josh Earnest declined to confirm on Friday whether the U.S. pays other countries to accept transfers from Guantanamo Bay, a terrorist prison, despite being asked directly twice. The Obama administration recently transferred 15 prisoners, leaving 61 detainees at the Cuba facility. The 12 Yemenis and three Afghans were sent to the United Arab Emirates. Since taking office in 2009, President Obama has consistently aimed to close Guantanamo. Fox News reporter Kevin Corke, after Earnest stated he had no advance notice of any further releases, inquired whether the U.S. pays a "fee" to countries willing to accept detainees. Earnest responded, "The process involves the U.S. working effectively with our international partners. A variety of countries have cooperated with us to ensure the safe and successful transfer of these individuals. Extensive diplomatic negotiations are involved, and in most cases, there are detailed discussions about the security measures imposed to prevent these individuals from posing a threat to the U.S." The White House has acknowledged that some released Guantanamo prisoners have returned to terrorist activities. The Washington Post reported in June that the administration believed at least 12 former detainees had killed a half-dozen Americans in Afghanistan alone. Corke reiterated his question, as Earnest had not directly addressed it. "Does the U.S. government pay these specific countries to take these detainees?" Corke asked. Earnest replied, "The transfers result from extensive diplomatic negotiations, and many questions arise about the most suitable location to house these individuals and the best methods to ensure they don't pose an ongoing threat to the U.S. or its allies. However, we usually do not disclose the specific requirements imposed on these individuals upon their transfer."
Senator Bernie Sanders' victory in the Wisconsin Democratic Primary on Tuesday marks his sixth consecutive win over challenger and front-runner Hillary Clinton, demonstrating that Sanders remains a viable contender in the race, according to political journalist Tim Murphy. Murphy, a senior reporter for Mother Jones, stated, "The campaign is not waning; it's essentially as robust as it's ever been." Tuesday's triumph awarded Sanders 48 delegates, while Clinton secured 38. This leaves Sanders with a net gain of either 10 or 4 delegates, depending on whether Wisconsin superdelegates who have already pledged their support to Clinton are taken into account. In total, Clinton still maintains a lead of 210 pledged delegates post-Tuesday's election. However, Murphy asserts that while Sanders still lags behind Clinton, he views Tuesday's win as a significant one. "It underscores that Sanders remains a legitimate candidate, and Clinton has much work ahead if she aims to maintain her lead," said Murphy. David Martosko, the U.S. political editor for The Daily Mail online, concurred, but noted that Sanders' campaign is banking on some superdelegates switching allegiances from Hillary Clinton. "Sanders is almost on par with Hillary Clinton in terms of earned delegates, and if superdelegates weren't a factor, this would be a fiercely competitive race and we would be scrutinizing it as closely as the Republicans," said Martosko.
If you're anticipating ASUS to release Android Wear 2 for the ZenWatch 2, you might be in for a long wait. However, the brave may find an alternative solution. You could consider installing Android Wear 2 unofficially, but this involves flashing your smartwatch with a custom ROM called WebWear. Notably, this ROM was developed by members of the XDA community, specifically for the ASUS ZenWatch 2, and is currently in the second release of its alpha build. This installation will grant you access to Android Wear 2 on your smartwatch. However, using an alpha build comes with its drawbacks. For WebWear, there are issues such as the touchscreen on your smartwatch occasionally freezing, and the inability to launch the Google Assistant from the smartwatch. If you're willing to accept these limitations, you may download the ROM via the official thread (source link below). The software is programmed to send 20MB of logs daily to the developer, which aids in determining what functions effectively and what doesn't within the build. Here's hoping that WebWear progresses to a beta build and eventually a stable one in due time. SOURCE: XDA

House Minority Leader Nancy Pelosi (D-Calif.) on Sunday labeled sequestration as a "detrimental approach" and advocated for a balance between spending reductions and closing tax loopholes to prevent it. Pelosi dismissed GOP proposals for the sequester replacement to solely focus on increased spending cuts and entitlement reforms. In an interview with Fox News's Chris Wallace, Pelosi stated that it is misleading to claim there is a spending problem; rather, the budget deficit needs to be addressed. Pelosi further asserted that the deficit and debt are at "morally reprehensible levels" and require reduction. The Democratic House leader expressed support for a "comprehensive, ambitious plan" that aims to abolish subsidies for large oil companies and eliminate tax code loopholes. Pelosi argued that targeting tax subsidies is a more effective solution compared to additional cuts that may impact programs such as education. She emphasized, "Investment in education for the American people is the primary source of revenue for the U.S. Treasury, so we must recognize this." According to Pelosi, cuts in education, scientific research, and related areas are detrimental and are the areas affected by sequestration. Both Democrats and Republicans agree on avoiding sequestration, an across-the-board cut to both military and non-defense programs that takes effect on March 1 if no action is taken. However, Republicans argue that the bill should be replaced solely by spending cuts, while Democrats favor a combination of spending cuts and increased revenue. On Saturday, President Obama warned in his weekly address that sequestration would inflict a "severe blow" on middle-class families and the economy as a whole. Obama urged Congress to reach a compromise to avert the $85 billion in automatic cuts. On Friday, administrative aides warned that the cuts would negatively impact law enforcement, education programs, mental health services, and result in furloughs for thousands of government workers. However, Republicans counter that they agreed to new tax revenues in January's "fiscal cliff" deal and now expect the president to focus on spending cuts and entitlement reforms. Dash cam footage shows Horne pulling Castaldo from her car a few seconds after the stop. According to Castaldo, she felt surprised and fearful during the encounter. The Utah Highway Patrol (UHP) spokesperson, Col. Danny Fuhr, stated that Horne suspected Castaldo of being under the influence of alcohol during the early morning hours of July 18, as she had just left her job at an Ogden bar. Right now I'm considering moving out of Ogden." Fuhr defended the trooper's actions, stating that he was within his legal rights to stop Castaldo and remove her from the car when she refused. A man named Raymond Hartley Jr., who previously served a prison sentence for burglary, has been sentenced to one to two years in a state prison for violating his probation. This violation occurred when he was caught using a device called the Whizzinator, a fake penis and bladder, during court-ordered drug tests in Monroe County. Hartley's defense attorney, Anthony Rybak, argued that the probation officers' mocking comments about the size of Hartley's real penis made him feel emasculated, leading him to use the Whizzinator. However, Judge Michael Koury Jr. was not convinced and sentenced Hartley. It should be noted that Hartley had earlier admitted to a misdemeanor related to his possession of the Whizzinator, which can be purchased online with synthetic urine and heater packs. Hartley claimed that the urine in the Whizzinator was his and he used the device only because of the comments from his probation officers. Hartley expressed regret for his actions, stating that he would not have used the Whizzinator if he had known it would lead to new charges. He also mentioned his struggle with drug addiction and his clean status at the time of the incident. Assistant District Attorney John Obrecht countered Hartley's defense, stating, "Deceit is deceit." In response, Rybak compared his client's situation to a breast cancer survivor who wears a prosthetic breast. He argued that while it might be deceitful, it is not necessarily wrong. Judge Koury expressed hope that Hartley would receive the drug treatment he needs during his state prison sentence.
Airline lounges can be accessed by individuals who are able to fabricate a QR code. When computer security expert and frequent traveler Przemek Jaroszewski was denied entry to an airline lounge in Warsaw due to a misreading of his boarding pass by an automated system, he developed a 600-line JavaScript program that generated a QR code for a business-class traveler named "Batholemew Simpson," who was scheduled to depart on the same day. This method was successful and continued to work. At Defcon, Jaroszewski demonstrated how he utilized this technique to gain entry to lounges throughout Europe, taking advantage of the fact that the lounge entry systems did not cross-reference entrants with the passenger manifests of the aircraft. Ten years ago, computer science student Chris Soghoian attracted an FBI visit by creating a boarding pass generator that would bypass airport security. Soghoian is now the chief technologist for the ACLU, and US aviation checkpoints now verify boarding passes by checking for a cryptographic signature from the TSA. This means that Jaroszewski's hack will grant ticketed, screened passengers access to lounges, but not random individuals into airports. Jaroszewski refuses to disclose his source code due to fear of an FBI visit, but claims it is simple enough to replicate. He has not attempted his attack against US airport lounges. As we move through airports, we often do not question why our boarding passes are scanned at various points. While it is done for the sake of passenger security, the security of boarding passes has been demonstrated to be compromised by researchers who have easily created their own passes, bypassing not only passenger-only screening, but also no-fly lists. Since then, not only have security problems persisted, but boarding passes have become increasingly bar-coded and are often checked by machines rather than humans. With a set of easily accessible tools, boarding pass hacking has become simpler than ever, and the checks are largely a security theater. In my presentation, I will delve into the creation, encoding, and validation of boarding pass information, and will demonstrate how simple it is to create a functional boarding pass that works at most checkpoints (and explain why it doesn't work at others). How can one secure a good seat in the security theater?

Modern technology, specifically robots, has been receiving unfavorable publicity recently. These machines, once thought to be taking over jobs, are now being questioned in various aspects. The Wall Street Journal reported that machine learning, a technique central to self-driving car development, has not shown significant success in stock market prediction. "Instances of parlaying machine learning into investing success over a sustained period are rare," the newspaper stated. In a similar vein, the safety and efficiency of self-driving vehicles have been called into question. An article in Scientific American titled "Deadly Tesla Crash Exposes Confusion over Automated Driving" highlights the challenges faced by these autonomous vehicles. Richard Waters, a Financial Times columnist, recently argued that while robots can excel in games like chess, they are unlikely to take over human jobs. He asserted that a computer is not about to replace a human worker. The world of robotics and artificial intelligence (AI) has also been exploring the realm of human intimacy, with early attempts met with mixed reviews. The British tabloids have been monitoring the progress of these ungainly, latex-clad automatae, with one imagined Yelp! review describing a mechanical encounter as "mechanical... it felt like making love to a robot." At a recent meeting of the Templeton Foundation-funded Cambridge Roundtable on Science and Religion, MIT professor Rosalind Picard discussed the topic "Emotional Artificial Intelligence and the Soul." Picard's presentation suggested that while robots can be programmed to display some emotions, they are currently devoid of a soul. She was particularly underwhelmed by the performance of the "social robot" Sophia on the Jimmy Fallon show, likening her to the Mechanical Turk, an 18th-century hoax machine, and a Cambridge meter maid. In conclusion, robots are struggling to make significant strides in stock market prediction, self-driving vehicles, newspaper column writing, human intimacy, and emotional intelligence. Despite these challenges, it seems that the widespread implementation of robots will occur after our lifetimes. The Simon Wiesenthal Center has levied accusations against the Lithuanian government, claiming it fosters the veneration of Holocaust-era war criminals. This accusation stems from a nationalist march held in Kaunas, Lithuania's second-largest city, where participants displayed portraits of Juozas Ambrazevicius-Brazaitis, a pro-Nazi former ruler whose administration aided German forces in deporting approximately 30,000 Jews to their deaths. The marchers also carried signs reading "Lithuania for Lithuanians." Efraim Zuroff, a representative from the center's Israel office, told JTA that attendance at the annual February 16 ultra-nationalist march significantly increased after Ambrazevicius-Brazaitis was reburied in Kaunas in 2012, a ceremony financed by the Lithuanian government. Prior to this, his remains were interred in Putnam, Connecticut, in the United States. Zuroff stated, "Last year there were 600 participants in this march. This year there were 1,000. This is a direct consequence of the government’s involvement in his glorification." Zuroff was in Kaunas to protest the rally alongside Dovid Katz, a Jewish academic based in Vilnius who is part of the Lithuanian Holocaust remembrance Defending History group. Approximately several dozen anti-fascist demonstrators also protested the march. Ambrazevicius-Brazaitis emigrated to the United States following the war and passed away there in 1974. He was honored in Lithuania in 2009 when then-President Valdas Adamkus bestowed upon him the Grand Cross of the Order of Vytautas the Great, Lithuania's highest state award, in recognition of his government's efforts to restore Lithuanian statehood after Soviet occupation. Between 1941 and 1944, up to 95% of Lithuania's 200,000-strong Jewish community perished at the hands of the Nazis and local collaborators.

Manage your Android device seamlessly from your desktop with SnapPea, a free utility that serves as an "iTunes-like" platform for Android phones, sans the poor performance. If you're in search of a comprehensive tool to oversee your Android device, SnapPea could be an excellent option to explore. Its primary appeal lies in its all-inclusive nature, offering a single application for managing all aspects of your phone. To use SnapPea, connect your phone to your PC via USB (ensure USB debugging is activated, as it employs ADB for file transfers and backups) or pair it with the app over Wi-Fi. Once connected, you can manage, organize, and transfer files to or from your Android device within the application's interface. You can install apps directly, bypassing Google Play if desired, update your phone's contacts, back up your device, manage photos, music, and videos, or import iTunes playlists and transfer your music. Perhaps its most remarkable feature is the ability to send and receive SMS messages on your desktop, using your Android phone. SnapPea's main competitor in this space is AirDroid, and while each has its advantages and disadvantages, the choice between them ultimately depends on your specific needs. SnapPea is available for free on Windows systems, and you can download the installer below. Unfortunately, there are no versions for OS X or Linux, but Mac users can download SnapPea's APK installer if needed for sideloading apps onto your Android device.
Stephen Paddock amassed a substantial collection of firearms prior to perpetrating the deadliest mass shooting in contemporary U.S. history in Las Vegas. The carnage and the extent of Paddock's arsenal, despite its apparent extremity, have rekindled the debate on gun control in the U.S. Questioning the prevalence of gun ownership among Americans and the demographics of these owners, we present an analysis of gun distribution in America: Do Americans own a significant number of guns? The answer is affirmative, both in absolute and relative terms. The U.S. leads the world in gun ownership, possessing approximately half of the global firearms while accounting for only 5% of the world's population. In per capita terms, the U.S. ranks first, with an estimated 89 to 100 guns for every 100 Americans in 2013, translating to roughly one firearm per person. Serbia ranks second with 75.6 guns per 100 people. To address the number of gun owners in America, a more nuanced approach is needed. According to data from the General Social Survey and Gallup in 2013, only a quarter of Americans are gun owners. However, it's important to note that many of these gun owners possess more than one firearm. The average American gun owner owns three firearms, as indicated by a 2015 survey conducted by Harvard and Northwestern University. Over half of them own one or two guns, while 14% of them—7.7 million or 3% of the U.S. population—own between eight and 140 firearms. This 3% of the population accounts for half of the civilian firearms in the U.S. The police discovered 47 firearms associated with Paddock in three different locations two days after the massacre, placing him among the 3%. It's worth noting that gun ownership surveys are subject to some degree of inaccuracy. Given that Americans can legally acquire firearms in many states without a permit or license, there is no consistent method for tracking the population of U.S. gun owners.

In the realm of contemporary communication, emojis and stickers have grown increasingly significant, and Google is joining the fray with a novel Allo feature. This feature, as reported by Fast Co Design, generates custom cartoon stickers from your selfies, crafting a digital avatar of you. Unlike other customizable tools such as Nintendo's Miis or Snapchat's Bitmoji, Google's version leverages machine learning and neural networks to match your facial features with animated versions designed by artist Lamar Abrams. These can then be further personalized. The end result is a collection of stickers showcasing your new cartoon likeness for use in future Allo conversations. A peek into Google's research blog sheds light on the intricate methodology behind this seemingly straightforward integration. The company discusses the measures taken to steer clear of the uncanny valley and ensure diverse representation across the generated illustrations, encompassing individuals of various races, genders, and age groups. As of today, the selfie-to-sticker tool is expected to launch for Allo on Android, with an iOS release imminent.

It appears that there's a widespread focus, particularly within the media, on whether hybrid/plug-in hybrid/electric vehicles will recoup their costs through fuel savings. The most recent instance of this is the misleading title, "The payoff for plug-in hybrids: 95 years?". It's understandable to scrutinize claims suggesting a specific vehicle will save money, but the emphasis on this aspect seems to have grown disproportionately. It's as if the primary purpose of eco-friendly transportation has been reduced to financial savings, serving as the benchmark for evaluating every new green vehicle. This reductionist view is preposterous. After all, when someone opts for a larger V6 engine, a sunroof, or better wheels, no one questions whether the investment will pay off. Instead, personal preferences and performance needs drive these decisions. On the other hand, individuals who choose cleaner cars due to lower emissions, the appeal of electric driving, or the encouragement of new clean technology, are often asked to present a financial case. Yet, the additional cost of a more efficient vehicle, while not completely recouped in all circumstances, does contribute to its overall cost. In contrast, a larger engine will only increase both the initial and ongoing costs. Let's abolish the financial double-standard for eco-friendly vehicles. If the question truly is, "will it pay for itself?", then by all means, let's discuss it. However, if we're to appreciate the broader benefits of these vehicles, such as reduced emissions, the joy of silent electric driving, and the promotion of cleaner technology, let's not make financial justification the sole criterion for their evaluation. For further reading, refer to: Green Basics: Hybrid-Electric Cars, Green Basics: Electric Vehicles and Cars.
The Governor of Research at De Nederlandsche Bank (DNB), Jakob de Haan, has provided fresh insights regarding the position of bitcoin within global finance. During a talk at the reinvent.money conference on September 26th, de Haan addressed several questions about bitcoin, including one that disparagingly referred to the euro as a "worthless fiat currency." Despite his position at the Dutch central bank, de Haan displayed an unusual frankness in his responses. In response to a question about whether his organization fears being replaced by decentralized financial technologies, such as bitcoin, de Haan indicated an open-minded approach, stating, "There are some who believe digital currencies are the future. Let's wait and see. I'm not opposed to these initiatives, and if you're right, the world will be very different, and I may lose my job, but by then I'll probably be retired." De Haan acknowledged that he is not an expert on bitcoin, but emphasized that the central bank is closely monitoring the emerging technology, with researchers actively exploring the subject. He clarified that while the bank is not fundamentally opposed to alternatives, it is important to note that digital currencies, when used as payment, do not offer the same consumer protections as traditional alternatives. This point was emphasized in May 2014, when the organization issued guidance on the matter. De Haan further noted that bitcoin's volatility against fiat currencies is a concern for its wider use. He emphasized the importance of price stability, stating, "Ordinary people benefit from price stability, and that is the most important mandate for central banks." Trustworthy news at your fingertips? Subscribe to our complimentary newsletters. I've hinted at this before, but it warrants a brief standalone post. There's been much discussion about Obamacare failing to meet the initial enrollment projections by the CBO in 2010, but it's not the only prediction they made. The CBO also projected that Obamacare would result in the loss of 8 million individuals from private insurance coverage by 2016. However, this didn't materialize. The individual mandate and employer mandate provisions of Obamacare actually prompted an increase in the purchase of individual coverage and employer coverage, respectively, leading to over 16 million more people having private coverage through mid-2015. As the graph on the right illustrates, private coverage, which had been around 61% of the population for four years, surged to 66% within a single year. While some of this increase might be attributed to the economic recovery, the pace of the economy has remained relatively consistent since 2012. The only significant change occurred in the fourth quarter of 2013, when Obamacare was introduced. In summary, while Obamacare may have fallen short of the CBO's exchange enrollment targets by approximately 7 million, it has exceeded their private insurance targets by a substantial 24 million. This is a positive development on multiple fronts as it's generally preferable for individuals to be insured by their employers rather than through the exchange. Not only does this offer better coverage, but it also saves taxpayers money. On any metric you care to consider, this is a significant and underreported success story.

Fiat Chrysler's shares experienced a significant decline on the Milan stock exchange, following media reports suggesting potential sales restrictions in Germany due to allegations of emissions test manipulation. Shares dropped by up to 5% initially, but later recuperated slightly. Over the weekend, Germany's Bild newspaper disclosed that German regulators had uncovered evidence of emissions-manipulating software in Fiat vehicles. This software, according to the newspaper's Sunday edition, deactivated emissions control technology after 22 minutes, two minutes beyond the standard test duration. The German Federal Motor Vehicle Office (KBA) reportedly discovered this during testing of a Fiat car and shared its findings with the European Commission and Italian authorities. As of yet, Fiat Chrysler has not issued a statement addressing these allegations. The company has also cancelled a scheduled meeting with German authorities, citing testing responsibility belonging to Italy. German transport minister, Alexander Dobrindt, has expressed disappointment at Fiat's uncooperative attitude, describing it as utterly incomprehensible.

The Royal College of Nursing (RCN) advocates for a distinct law to penalize verbal and physical abuse directed towards nurses who care for the public. Daily, approximately 193 incidents of aggression against UK healthcare workers are reported, with NHS Protect recording a 4% increase in physical assaults against healthcare workers in England, rising from 67,864 in 2014/15 to 70,555 in 2015/16. A survey of RCN members revealed that 56% had personally experienced physical or verbal abuse from patients, with an additional 63% reporting abuse from patients' relatives or members of the public. Following a petition signed by over 116,000 people, spearheaded by LBC radio presenter Nick Ferrari, there will be a parliamentary debate for Members of Parliament (MPs) to discuss the possibility of making it a specific criminal offense to attack a public healthcare professional. The hope is that a specific law would serve as a greater deterrent for those willing to verbally or physically assault nurses on the job. Annually, such incidents are estimated to cost the health service around £69 million due to staff absence, decreased productivity, and the need for additional security. In response to the petition, the Government stated, 'Assaults against NHS staff are unacceptable, and are already criminalized. If the victim serves the public, an assault is treated more seriously. A new offence would not provide additional protection.' Kathryn Yates, RCN Professional Lead for Primary and Community Care, expressed continued concern for the safety of lone-working nurses in the community. In 2015, half of all community nurses surveyed by the RCN reported feeling vulnerable at work, and nearly half stated that the risks had worsened due to NHS cost-cutting. Increasing caseloads, a lack of staff, and the resulting need for nurses to work alone pose significant risks. Additionally, several local councils have reduced their community nursing budgets by 50%, and the overall number of district nurses has decreased by 42%. Over 500 school districts across the nation have committed to correctly pronouncing each student's name due to a decision by several progressive education groups that mispronouncing names constitutes a "microaggression." This "microaggression" is one of many everyday words and actions that radical leftists deem worthy of anger or frustration. The initiative to pronounce every student's name correctly originated from a national campaign last year, led by the National Association for Bilingual Education and the publicly funded school district in Santa Clara, California, as reported by the Media Research Center. Known as "My Name, My Identity: A Declaration of Self," the campaign contends that teachers cause "anxiety and resentment" when they mispronounce students' names. The campaign offers teachers the opportunity to declare their names and pledge to "respect others' names and identities in schools by pronouncing students' names correctly," as well as to share name stories on social media and celebrate their individuality. Recently, Rita Kohli, an education professor at the University of California, Riverside, asserted that mispronouncing a student's name can impede the learning process. She explained that names hold significant meaning to families, and when teachers inadvertently or intentionally mispronounce, disregard, or alter a student's name, they are essentially disregarding the student's family and culture. Kohli's online resume reveals a frequent use of words related to race, with "race," "racial," "racially," "multiracial," "interracial," and "racialization" appearing approximately 60 times. In a 2014 post on the website "Cult of Pedagogy," it was argued that mispronouncing names is equivalent to "mutilating someone's name" and "a tiny act of bigotry." The author, Jennifer Gonzalez, stated that while most students may not understand the term "microaggression," they are likely familiar with feelings of marginalization and the message that everyone else is "normal," while they are not. Grindeanu plans to hold an extraordinary Cabinet meeting on Sunday to explore a legal means to prevent the decree from being enacted. Approximately 120,000 individuals took to the streets of Romania for the fifth consecutive day on Saturday, protesting against a decree that eases penalties for official misconduct. Demonstrators marched through Bucharest, culminating at the parliament building where they formed a human chain. The previous day, between 200,000 and 250,000 people protested across the country. The government's decision to revoke the decree was not entirely surprising, as the leader of Grindeanu's Social Democrats (PSD) had hinted earlier on Saturday that the government might reconsider the decree. Grindeanu reiterated that the decree would not come into effect. Calin Tariceanu, head of ALDE party - a junior partner of the ruling Social Democrat coalition - stated on private TV station Romania TV on Saturday that the decision was made because "we have nothing to hide." Romania's Constitutional Court is scheduled to rule next week on the legality of the measure. The decree proposes to decriminalize abuse-of-power offenses for sums below 200,000 lei ($47,500; 44,200 euros). It also redefines conflict of interest, making it legal for a public official to favor a business partner when deciding who should win a contract. Grindeanu's left-leaning government, in power for less than a month, also aims to release around 2,500 prisoners who are serving sentences of less than five years. The intricate process of minting money leaves room for numerous errors, resulting in a plethora of unusual mistakes that can befall a banknote. The more errors present on a single note, the rarer and more valuable it becomes. Let's explore some notable examples of misprinted U.S. currency and calculate the impact of these errors on their worth. $1 Bill with Inverted Obverse A keen observer may notice something amiss in this $1 bill. With the eye of God appearing to descend, the effect is almost demonic. Value: $760.5 Increase: 75,950% $20 Star Shift This $20 bill features a subtle error – the star to the left of the 7th U.S. President, Andrew Jackson, is drifting towards his head, resembling a throwing star in a ninja assassination attempt. Upon closer inspection, both serial numbers, the treasury seal, and all four district numbers have also shifted to the right. Value: $643 Increase: 3,115% $1 Multiple Gutter Folds Unexpected creases and folds have created an interesting pattern of lines on this $1 bill, as if George Washington had attended an exciting laser show. The depositions were taken between December 10 and January 21. Trump is being sued by former students who allege they were defrauded by Trump University. The case is being presided over by Judge Gonzalo Curiel, whom Trump has criticized for his "Mexican heritage." The media companies maintain that the strong public interest in this case, which involves allegations of fraud that have become a significant issue in the 2016 presidential campaign, warrants the disclosure of materials produced during the litigation. During her visit to China and Hong Kong for trade purposes, she emphasized that promoting gender equality could significantly reshape the global economy. Haribo, renowned for its gummy bears and other confectioneries, will establish its first U.S. factory in Wisconsin. Governor Scott Walker announced this on Thursday, stating that the Kenosha County plant will create approximately 400 jobs and is set to open in 2020. This investment from a foreign company is one of the largest in Wisconsin's history, according to the governor's office. The new facility, spanning 500,000 square feet, will be the third-largest confectionery plant in the U.S. Construction is expected to commence next year, with an initial capital investment of $242 million. Mark Maley, Public Affairs and Communications Director for Wisconsin Economic Development Corporation, made this announcement. Governor Walker expressed gratitude towards Haribo for selecting Wisconsin, praising its business-friendly climate and strong fiscal management. Maley added that discussions about the project between Wisconsin and Haribo began about six months ago. Haribo is famous for its Gold-Bears, fruit-flavored gummy bears, as well as gummy Coca-Cola bottles, licorice, sour peaches, and other treats. The family-run candy maker currently employs nearly 7,000 people globally and operates 16 manufacturing firms in 10 countries. Kenosha County Executive Jim Kreuser stated that the manufacturing plant will have a significant impact on both the country and the state, calling it a "sweet day" for Kenosha County.

Volkswagen has disclosed that approximately 800,000 vehicles, primarily equipped with 1.4-liter diesel engines, may exhibit inconsistencies in their carbon dioxide (CO2) emissions. This revelation, concerning vehicles across Europe, could potentially represent a global number significantly higher than the stated figure. Originally, the company was embroiled in a scandal surrounding the excessive production of nitrous oxides (NOx) by diesel engines, a harmful pollutant. VW had employed two methods to reduce NOx emissions, including catalytic reduction with urea and, as demonstrated, deceptive practices during emissions testing. Now, it appears that the cars in question may also be underperforming in terms of CO2 emissions during real-world use compared to the emissions certification testing. Volkswagen's statement explains, "During the course of internal investigations, unexplained inconsistencies were found when determining Type Approval CO2 levels." The cars affected include the VW Polo, Golf, and some models from Audi, Seat, and Skoda. Additionally, 1.4-liter petrol engines may also be impacted, but further details are not yet available. If it is discovered that VW manipulated CO2 data to present higher mileage and lower emissions than actual real-world usage, the company could face substantial regulatory fines due to the close relationship between CO2 emissions and fuel consumption. Volkswagen has already allocated €2 billion (approximately £1.4 billion or $2.16 billion) to address the potential CO2 issues for the affected 800,000 vehicles. This is approximately four times the amount set aside for the NOx defeat device issue. In light of these CO2 irregularities, Volkswagen has reported a 10 percent drop in its share price since the announcement was made late last night. The company has previously written off €6.5 billion ($7.05 billion) for the 11 million vehicles affected by the NOx emissions scandal. If the CO2 issue expands beyond the current 800,000 cars, Volkswagen could face additional write-offs of tens of billions of euros.

In the present, one can arrange a wine-tasting journey to Washington state, or alternatively, consider scheduling it in several decades. Owing to increasing temperatures, the Pacific Northwest might expand by approximately 80,000 square miles of vineyard-friendly land, a significant portion of which currently serves as habitat for grizzly bears and wolves. However, in this future warming scenario, most wine grape varieties may find it more challenging to thrive in around 40% of California's current vineyard regions. This prediction comes from Lee Hannah, a biologist with Conservation International, who employed three methods to evaluate wine grape suitability and map the impact of climate change on global viticultural regions. Regions such as Mediterranean Europe, South Africa, and Australia may also face challenges, while areas with higher latitudes and elevations, like parts of New Zealand and northern Europe, may experience an advantage. While vineyards will likely adapt through methods such as shadier trellising and planting heat-tolerant varietals, the production of certain varieties, like Zinfandel, may increase. On the contrary, it will become progressively harder to cultivate wine grapes in certain regions, while areas that were previously challenging may become more suitable.

Chanel Dubofsky, a 37-year-old writer who divides her time between New York and Massachusetts, attributes her overachieving tendencies to her formative years. This was exacerbated when her mother was diagnosed with cancer at age 7, and later relapsed twice, at ages 13 and 17. During her middle school years, she would wake up in the middle of the night with severe headaches until she had completed her homework. Her high school peers fostered this work style, engaging in an informal competition over who was getting the least sleep. Dubofsky's workaholism intensified during the college semester when her mother passed away. Despite taking 20 credits' worth of classes while most students took 12 or 15, she would stay up all night to complete her assignments and sleep until noon. She managed to maintain high grades, even in morning classes she often skipped due to exhaustion, only disclosing her recent loss to professors much later. "I was aware of the societal expectations regarding grief behavior, and I wasn't meeting any of them," she admits. Research on mental health suggests that grief can manifest in various ways, such as anger, guilt, depression, or reduced productivity at work. However, unhealthy responses to grief can include making significant life changes or attempting to suppress or evade one's feelings. While work can serve as a means to alleviate stress when done in moderation, and can provide purpose and meaning, individuals with traumatic stress or PTSD may channel or avoid unresolved feelings through overwork, becoming so engrossed in work that they overlook physical discomfort or other distress, as psychotherapist Bryan Robinson explains. A 29-year-old nonprofit worker in New York, who prefers to remain anonymous to protect her privacy, identifies as a trauma survivor and workaholic. "Constantly working and being in a state of motion is one way I avoid confronting my emotions," she confesses. Rebekah Reysen, a researcher at The University of Mississippi who co-authored an article on workaholism in teachers, agrees, stating, "Overworking can serve as an escape: people can run towards something as they run away from something else." Over 2,600 individuals are still unaccounted for in Japan, three years after the country was struck by a devastating earthquake, tsunami, and nuclear disaster. As per the latest data from the National Police Agency, the majority of these missing persons are from the affected prefectures of Miyagi, Iwate, and Fukushima. The death toll from the disaster, which occurred on March 11, 2011, stands at 15,884. Additionally, the identities of 98 deceased individuals remain undetermined. Although life in many affected communities appears to have resumed some semblance of normalcy, with debris cleared and businesses reopened, the struggles of those whose loved ones remain missing persist. Monthly searches are carried out on the 11th of each month by police officers, Maritime Safety Agency personnel, and local volunteers at the behest of the missing persons' relatives. One such individual is Yasuo Takamatsu, a 57-year-old resident of the small fishing town of Onagawa, Miyagi prefecture, who lost his wife, Yuko, in the tsunami. In an effort to find his missing spouse, Mr. Takamatsu recently learned to scuba dive and has been searching the Pacific Ocean floor for any signs of her. Mrs. Takamatsu, a bank worker, was one of 250 people still missing from Onagawa, a town severely impacted by the disaster, with over 800 lives claimed by the 65-foot tsunami. In her final text message to her husband after the earthquake, Yuko expressed her desire to return home, saying simply "I want to go home." A later, unsent message found on her phone read, "Tsunami huge." Mr. Takamatsu lamented, "That was the last message from her. I feel terrible thinking she is still out there. I want to bring her home as soon as possible."
Drivers might have witnessed their lowest prices yet, as analysts predict gas prices could drop below $3 by fall. In the past month, gas prices have already decreased by 6%, and since April, they've dropped by $0.49. Oil speculators like Tom Kloza believe this downward trend will continue, with predictions of gas prices below $3 by Halloween and certainly by Thanksgiving, as stated to USA Today. The lower fuel costs come at a perfect time, as we approach the peak summer driving season. AAA anticipates that 42.3 million Americans will travel 50 miles or more during the Fourth of July weekend, representing a nearly 5% increase in travel compared to last year, with 35 million choosing to travel by car. Currently, the national average gas price stands at $3.45 per gallon, and crude oil prices have fallen to $78 a barrel, the lowest since late winter, according to reports from West Texas Intermediate.

Marvel Comics is officially concluding the Luke Cage series. The future of the ongoing Luke Cage comic book series had been uncertain following its absence in Marvel Comics' March 2018 solicitations. Writer David F. Walker has since confirmed that Luke Cage #170 will indeed be the series' final issue, tweeting, "Sad but true...Luke Cage (the comic book, not the TV series) has been cancelled." He further elaborated on the reasons for the cancellation and the current state of the comic book industry, stating, "Luke Cage was cancelled because it sold poorly. VERY poorly. The success of superheroes in film, television, and video games only translates to comics when people actually purchase the comics. The truth is that not many people buy comics. Of the top 10 best-selling comics in November, only four sold more than 100,000 units. That's sad." Luke Cage joins a list of six other Marvel series that have been canceled, including Generation X, Hawkeye, Iceman, Jean Grey, and The Unbelievable Gwenpool. Secret Warriors and Defenders, like these series, are also absent from Marvel's March 2018 solicitations, although their cancellations have not yet been confirmed. Matthew Rosenberg, writer of Secret Warriors, has commented on the recent wave of cancellations, stating, "There is no bloodbath of cancellations. Some books end so new ones can take their place. That's just the cycle." The Mighty Captain Marvel and Champions, which had raised concerns due to their absence in the March solicitations and a seemingly hinted team breakup, have been confirmed to continue. Margaret Stohl, writer of Captain Marvel, assured fans, "Concerned Citizens: nope Captain Marvel has not been canceled & we are hard at work on all things Carol Danvers even now!" Mark Waid similarly dispelled concerns about Champions, stating, "Book's not going away, not cancelled. No worries." Recently, Guardians of the Galaxy was also confirmed as canceled, making way for the Infinity Countdown series.
Approximately half of Scots indicate they are sufficiently informed about the issues pertaining to independence and the upcoming referendum, according to research conducted by Yes Scotland. A survey conducted by the pro-independence group disclosed that 46% of those surveyed believe they are adequately informed prior to the 2014 vote. Of this group, 47% expressed intentions to vote in favor, slightly trailing the 48% who expressed plans to vote against. The survey, part of a larger research program by Yes Scotland, questioned over a thousand individuals in April. It also revealed that 74% of people are engaged in the independence debate, as opposed to 10% who expressed disinterest. Furthermore, 75% stated they are highly likely to vote, with an additional 13% indicating they are somewhat likely to vote. Blair Jenkins, CEO of Yes Scotland, stated: "These surveys are but one component of our extensive research program. They, along with other comprehensive analyses, suggest that the more people understand about independence, the more they are drawn to it. They indicate that if we persist in our efforts and effectively communicate our message to a wider audience, we stand a strong chance. With less than a year remaining until the referendum, the figures suggest that we are currently running even with the No campaign among those who are already well-informed."

AMD has unexpectedly altered the codename of its upcoming high-end Radeon GPU family, switching from "Pirate Islands" to "Caribbean Islands" at the eleventh hour. While not exactly at the last minute, rumors suggest the new cards will hit the market as early as summer, according to Fudzilla. The online publication claims its sources are reliable regarding such matters. However, release dates can be notoriously difficult to predict with so much development time remaining, so it's advisable to approach the "June to August" timeframe with a healthy dose of skepticism. Should AMD's arch-rival Nvidia unveil an early 2015 GeForce GTX 990 launch, pressure might mount on AMD to expedite the production of Caribbean Islands GPUs. The anticipated successor to the already impressive GTX 980 flagship could widen the performance gap between Nvidia and AMD, potentially forcing AMD's hand. As for the production process of the Radeon R9 290 and 295X2 successors, AMD is rumored to be considering a reduction in nanometer count from 28 to 20. A direct jump to a 16nm FinFET die is not implausible, given the later release date. However, accelerated R&D might be necessary to make such a production leap possible. When AMD unveils its next generation of graphics processing units, we anticipate a grand reveal in the actual Caribbean islands. This isn't without precedent, as the company did the same for its "Hawaii" GPU. Interestingly, several Caribbean island names have been used in the past to designate Radeon products, such as Cayman XT for the HD 6970 and Antilles XT for the Radeon HD 6990. With numerous options still available, possibilities include Antigua, Bahamas, Belize, or Aruba. A sunny, tropical, cocktail-filled vacation might be on the horizon for some. Google is set to release its self-driving prototype bubble car onto California's public roads this summer. A select number of these vehicles will be circulating in Mountain View. To alleviate concerns about safety, especially in light of recent questions about Google's safety record, the cars will always have a human operator on board, and will be equipped with removable steering wheel, accelerator pedal, and brake pedal for manual control if necessary. The vehicles will be restricted to a maximum speed of 25mph. Google's self-driving Lexus RX450h SUV fleet, which has been on California's streets since September of last year, has accumulated nearly a million autonomous miles and currently clocks up around 10,000 miles per week. Google claims that the total miles driven autonomously by their fleet equates to "75 years of typical American adult driving experience." The prototype bubble cars will leverage the software and experience from the Lexus fleet during public testing. Google views this public testing as a crucial step towards enabling the cars to drive independently. It's also a significant milestone for public perception and acceptance, as self-driving cars face concerns about safety despite evidence to the contrary. Public tests that allow interaction with the cars may help allay those fears. Google responded proactively this week after it was revealed that four out of the 50 self-driving cars on California's roads had been involved in accidents, with three of the four being Google's own Lexus SUVs. Chris Urmson, a Google representative, wrote on Medium that over the past 6 years, Google's self-driving project has been involved in 11 minor accidents (light damage, no injuries) during 1.7 million miles of autonomous and manual driving with safety drivers behind the wheel, and not once was the self-driving car the cause of the accident.

In 2014, the Chief Rabbinate of Israel reported a 2% decrease in the divorce rate, with the number of divorces dropping from 11,249 in 2013 to 11,023. However, both Jerusalem and Tel Aviv saw an increase in divorces, with Jerusalem leading the way with 874 divorces in 2014, compared to 733 in 2013, and Tel Aviv following closely behind with 779 divorces in 2014, compared to 678 in 2013. Haifa came in third place with 629 divorces, while Petach Tikvah had 439. Azor had the fewest divorces, with 29. In total, 89,551 divorce cases were opened in the Rabbanut system in 2014, compared to 90,237 in the previous year. Of these cases, 7,541 were initiated by couples seeking a divorce, 3,437 were opened for alimony, 3,341 for inheritance matters, and 2,501 for child custody. The unit responsible for freeing agunos (women who are unable to remarry because their husbands refuse to grant them a divorce) reported that it secured a get (a Jewish divorce) for 184 agunos in 2014, compared to 159 in 2013. The batei din (rabbinical courts) imposed sanctions on 40 recalcitrant husbands in 191 cases, with the husband fleeing the country in 184 of those cases. Sanctions included liens on bank accounts and assets, prevention from leaving the country, and revocation of a driver's license or employment in the public sector. If imprisoned, such individuals would face harsher conditions than convicted criminals. The batei din system issued 16 arrest warrants for recalcitrant husbands, compared to 19 in 2013, and protective orders in 152 cases to protect wives from violence, compared to 140 in 2013. Smartphones are transforming into remote controls for streetlights in a German village's initiative to lower energy bills and emissions. In Dörentrup, a village 60 miles from Hanover, residents now have the power to turn on street lamps as needed, simply by making a call. The village has successfully trialed the Dial4Light scheme on several streets for a year and plans to expand it throughout the settlement and beyond. All 9,000 residents can register for the free system, allowing them to dial a code displayed on a lamp post, activating the lights within seconds. The lights remain on for up to 15 minutes before automatically shutting off. The mayor of Dörentrup, Friedrich Ehlert, stated that the primary goal is to save money but also aims to raise the community's awareness of energy usage. The idea originated after the village council made a difficult decision to turn off the lights at night due to financial constraints. A local resident proposed the solution to make the lights available on-demand, which was taken seriously and developed into a special modem and software by the county council in Lemgo, with expert assistance. Data collected by the council indicates that the Dial4Light system will result in an annual reduction of nearly 20 tonnes in the community's carbon dioxide emissions, equivalent to the emissions of 11 four-person households. The project has generated interest from numerous European countries, Saudi Arabia, Japan, India, and the US, with Lemgo set to launch the Dial4Light system internationally later this month.

In an interview scheduled for broadcast on ARD public television on Sunday, German President Joachim Gauck expressed discomfort over the possibility of Bodo Ramelow, the Left party's potential premier of Thuringia. Gauck questioned whether the party had so fundamentally distanced itself from the repressive ideologies of the East German communist party (SED) that they could be fully trusted. He noted that there are aspects of the party that he, like many others, finds difficult to trust. Despite his reservations, Gauck acknowledged the legitimacy of the results from the recent Thuringia state election. The United States military has acknowledged utilizing depleted uranium (DU) ammunition in Syria, a contentious weaponry known to induce severe health issues among the populace. Reports indicated that in late 2015, during strikes against alleged Daesh (ISIL) positions in Syria, the military discharged thousands of rounds filled with mutagenic depleted uranium. Weapons experts assert that the deployment of depleted uranium constitutes a war crime, a violation of human rights, and an act of genocide due to the civilian population's exposure to depleted uranium resulting in genetic damage, birth defects, cancer, immune system dysfunction, and other serious health problems. U.S. Central Command (CENTCOM) spokesman Maj. Josh Jacques confirmed the use of depleted uranium, stating that a mix of Armored Piercing Incendiary (DU) rounds and High Explosive Incendiary rounds were employed to enhance the likelihood of destroying ISIS's [Daesh] oil transport vehicles. Jacques revealed that a total of 5,265 armor-piercing 30mm rounds containing depleted uranium were discharged on November 16 and November 22, 2015, annihilating approximately 250 vehicles. On November 16, about 1,490 out of 1,790 rounds fired contained depleted uranium, and on November 22, around 3,775 out of 4,530 rounds were laced with the substance. Despite previous promises to abstain from using depleted uranium weapons on the battlefield in Iraq and Syria, the U.S. military had initially declared in March 2015 that U.S. and coalition aircraft would not employ depleted uranium munitions in Iraq or Syria during Operation Inherent Resolve. Later that month, the Pentagon stated that the controversial weaponry would not be used against Daesh as the terrorist group did not possess the tanks the weapons were designed to penetrate. An in-depth, critical examination of the renowned video game series, BioShock, is presented through philosophical, ideological, and computational lenses, focusing on the analysis of systems, choices, and propaganda. Reviews & Endorsements One intriguing aspect of New Materialism lies in the use of digital cultural artifacts such as video games for philosophical contemplation. A prime example of this is Robert Jackson's book, Bioshock: Decision, Forced Choice, and Propaganda. In this concise publication, Jackson employs the video game franchise BioShock to explore questions of choice, causality, and decision-making, not only within the gameplay but also in broader societal contexts. ~ Charlie Gere, Oxford University Press Journals: Years Work in Critical and Cultural Theory, vol 23, no 1, 2015 Robert Jackson skillfully steers clear of the superficiality often associated with studies of the aesthetic and cultural impact of video games, instead offering a comprehensive critique of the ideology of decision in modern society. He posits that decision-making is not a personal ability, but a systemic and collective one. ~ Ian Bogost: Ivan Allen College Distinguished Chair in Media Studies and Professor of Interactive Computing at the Georgia Institute of Technology., Author of Unit Operations: An Approach to Videogame Criticism, Persuasive Games: The Expressive Power of Videogames, and How To Do Things with Videogames It's essential to understand that this book is not solely about a video game franchise. Rather, it delves into the world of power dynamics and compulsory choices, examining the dichotomy between decision and choice, and the intricate nested structures that define our contemporary political landscape. It reveals how the notion of the self is already predetermined by external factors. Decisions are automated, algorithmic, and systemic, forming a constant stream. Robert Jackson's insightful book sheds light on this critical moment through BioShock in a thought-provoking manner. ~ Jussi Parikka: Reader in Media & Design, at University of Southampton, Winchester School of Art., Author of Digital Contagions: A Media Archaeology of Computer Viruses, Insect Media, and What is Media Archaeology?
